<th scope="row">Waterways</th>
<td><a href="/wiki/St._Johns_River" title="St. Johns River">St. Johns River</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trout_River_(Florida)" title="Trout River (Florida)">Fall Creek</a>, Arlington River</td>

<tr class="mergedrow">
<th scope="row">Airports</th>
<td><a href="/wiki/Jacksonville_International_Airport" title="Jacksonville International Airport">Jacksonville International Airport</a></td>
</tr>
<tr class="mergedtoprow">
<th scope="row">Website</th>
<td><a class="external text" href="http://www.coj.net" rel="nofollow">City of Jacksonville</a></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Jacksonville</b> is the largest city by population in the U.S. state of <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_cities_by_area" title="List of United States cities by area">largest city by area</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Contiguous_United_States" title="Contiguous United States">contiguous United States</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> It is the <a href="/wiki/County_seat" title="County seat">county seat</a> of <a href="/wiki/Duval_County,_Florida" title="Duval County, Florida">Duval County</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GR6_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-GR6-10">[10]</a></sup> with which the city government <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville_Consolidation" title="Jacksonville Consolidation">consolidated</a> in 1968. Consolidation gave Jacksonville its great size and placed most of its <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_area" title="Metropolitan area">metropolitan population</a> within the city limits; with an estimated population of 913,010 as of 2017, Jacksonville is the most populous city in the state of Florida<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JBJcensus_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-JBJcensus-11">[11]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_United_States" title="Southeastern United States">southeastern United States</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville_metropolitan_area" title="Jacksonville metropolitan area">Jacksonville metropolitan area</a> has a population of 1,626,611. It is the 34th largest MSA in the United States and fourth largest in Florida.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jacksonville is centered on the banks of the <a href="/wiki/St._Johns_River" title="St. Johns River">St. Johns River</a> in the <a href="/wiki/First_Coast" title="First Coast">First Coast</a> region of northeast Florida, about 25 miles (40 km) south of the <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a> state line and 340 miles (550 km) north of <a href="/wiki/Miami" title="Miami">Miami</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville_Beaches" title="Jacksonville Beaches">Jacksonville Beaches</a> communities are along the adjacent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlantic" title="Atlantic">Atlantic</a> coast. The area was originally inhabited by the <a href="/wiki/Timucua" title="Timucua">Timucua</a> people, and in 1564 was the site of the French colony of <a href="/wiki/Fort_Caroline" title="Fort Caroline">Fort Caroline</a>, one of the earliest European settlements in what is now the continental United States. Under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Florida" title="British Florida">British rule</a>, settlement grew at the narrow point in the river where cattle crossed, known as <i>Wacca Pilatka</i> to the <a href="/wiki/Seminole" title="Seminole">Seminole</a> and the Cow Ford to the British. A platted town was established there in 1822, a year after the United States gained <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Florida" title="Spanish Florida">Florida</a> from Spain; it was named after <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>, the first <a href="/wiki/List_of_Governors_of_Florida" title="List of Governors of Florida">military governor</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Territory" title="Florida Territory">Florida Territory</a> and seventh <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President of the United States</a>.</p>
<p>Harbor improvements since the late 19th century have made Jacksonville a major military and civilian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep-water_port" title="Deep-water port">deep-water port</a>. Its riverine location facilitates <a href="/wiki/Naval_Station_Mayport" title="Naval Station Mayport">Naval Station Mayport</a>, <a href="/wiki/Naval_Air_Station_Jacksonville" title="Naval Air Station Jacksonville">Naval Air Station Jacksonville</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Marine_Corps" title="U.S. Marine Corps">U.S. Marine Corps</a> <a href="/wiki/Blount_Island_Command" title="Blount Island Command">Blount Island Command</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Port_of_Jacksonville" title="Port of Jacksonville">Port of Jacksonville</a>, Florida's third largest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seaport" title="Seaport">seaport</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AAPA_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-AAPA-14">[14]</a></sup> Jacksonville's military bases and the nearby <a href="/wiki/Naval_Submarine_Base_Kings_Bay" title="Naval Submarine Base Kings Bay">Naval Submarine Base Kings Bay</a> form the third largest military presence in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Port_of_Jacksonville_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Port_of_Jacksonville-15">[15]</a></sup> Significant factors in the local economy include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Service_sector" title="Service sector">services</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banking" title="Banking">banking</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insurance_industry" title="Insurance industry">insurance</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Healthcare" title="Healthcare">healthcare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Logistics" title="Logistics">logistics</a>. As with much of Florida, <a href="/wiki/Tourism" title="Tourism">tourism</a> is also important to the Jacksonville area, particularly tourism related to <a href="/wiki/Golf" title="Golf">golf</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-17">[17]</a></sup> People from Jacksonville may be called "Jacksonvillians" or "Jaxsons" (also spelled "Jaxons").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FTUname_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-FTUname-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jaxson_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jaxson-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#Infrastructure"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Infrastructure</span></a>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacksonville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Jacksonville,_Florida" title="History of Jacksonville, Florida">History of Jacksonville, Florida</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Timeline of Jacksonville, Florida">Timeline of Jacksonville, Florida</a></div>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: left; clear: left; margin: 0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0; width:22em; padding: 10px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 90%; background-color: #B0C4DE;">
<div class="" style="background-color: #B0C4DE; color: black; text-align: center; font-size: larger; font-weight: bold;">Historical affiliations</div>
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Royal_Standard_of_the_King_of_France.svg/23px-Royal_Standard_of_the_King_of_France.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Royal_Standard_of_the_King_of_France.svg/35px-Royal_Standard_of_the_King_of_France.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Royal_Standard_of_the_King_of_France.svg/45px-Royal_Standard_of_the_King_of_France.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></span> <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_France" title="Kingdom of France">Kingdom of France</a> 1562–1565<br/>
<span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="750" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Flag_of_Cross_of_Burgundy.svg/23px-Flag_of_Cross_of_Burgundy.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Flag_of_Cross_of_Burgundy.svg/35px-Flag_of_Cross_of_Burgundy.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Flag_of_Cross_of_Burgundy.svg/45px-Flag_of_Cross_of_Burgundy.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></span> <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish Empire</a> 1565–1763<br/>
<span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1000" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Union_flag_1606_%28Kings_Colors%29.svg/23px-Union_flag_1606_%28Kings_Colors%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Union_flag_1606_%28Kings_Colors%29.svg/35px-Union_flag_1606_%28Kings_Colors%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Union_flag_1606_%28Kings_Colors%29.svg/46px-Union_flag_1606_%28Kings_Colors%29.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></span> <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Great Britain</a> 1763–1783<br/>
<span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="750" height="15" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Flag_of_Cross_of_Burgundy.svg/23px-Flag_of_Cross_of_Burgundy.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Flag_of_Cross_of_Burgundy.svg/35px-Flag_of_Cross_of_Burgundy.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Flag_of_Cross_of_Burgundy.svg/45px-Flag_of_Cross_of_Burgundy.svg.png 2x" width="23"/></span> <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">Spanish Empire</a> 1783–1821<br/>
<span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="1710" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States_%281848-1851%29.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_United_States_%281848-1851%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States_%281848-1851%29.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_United_States_%281848-1851%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Flag_of_the_United_States_%281848-1851%29.svg/46px-Flag_of_the_United_States_%281848-1851%29.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> 1821–1861<br/>

</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacksonville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NortheastFLRomans.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="232" data-file-width="491" height="104" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/NortheastFLRomans.png/220px-NortheastFLRomans.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/NortheastFLRomans.png/330px-NortheastFLRomans.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/NortheastFLRomans.png/440px-NortheastFLRomans.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NortheastFLRomans.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Northeast Florida showing Cow Ford (center) from <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Romans" title="Bernard Romans">Bernard Romans</a>' 1776 map of Florida.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The area of the modern city of Jacksonville has been inhabited for thousands of years. On Black Hammock Island in the national <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timucuan_Ecological_and_Historic_Preserve" title="Timucuan Ecological and Historic Preserve">Timucuan Ecological and Historic Preserve</a>, a <a href="/wiki/University_of_North_Florida" title="University of North Florida">University of North Florida</a> team discovered some of the oldest remnants of pottery in the United States, dating to 2500 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FTU_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FTU-18">[18]</a></sup> In the 16th century, the beginning of the historical era, the region was inhabited by the <a href="/wiki/Mocama" title="Mocama">Mocama</a>, a coastal subgroup of the <a href="/wiki/Timucua" title="Timucua">Timucua</a> people. At the time of contact with Europeans, all Mocama villages in present-day Jacksonville were part of the powerful chiefdom known as the <a href="/wiki/Saturiwa" title="Saturiwa">Saturiwa</a>, centered around the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/St._Johns_River" title="St. Johns River">St. Johns River</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> One early map shows a village called <i>Ossachite</i> at the site of what is now downtown Jacksonville; this may be the earliest recorded name for that area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jacksonville_FL_Fort_Caroline_Natl_Mem_Ribault_mnmt_detail01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="2448" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Jacksonville_FL_Fort_Caroline_Natl_Mem_Ribault_mnmt_detail01.jpg/170px-Jacksonville_FL_Fort_Caroline_Natl_Mem_Ribault_mnmt_detail01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Jacksonville_FL_Fort_Caroline_Natl_Mem_Ribault_mnmt_detail01.jpg/255px-Jacksonville_FL_Fort_Caroline_Natl_Mem_Ribault_mnmt_detail01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Jacksonville_FL_Fort_Caroline_Natl_Mem_Ribault_mnmt_detail01.jpg/340px-Jacksonville_FL_Fort_Caroline_Natl_Mem_Ribault_mnmt_detail01.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jacksonville_FL_Fort_Caroline_Natl_Mem_Ribault_mnmt_detail01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Replica of <a href="/wiki/Jean_Ribault" title="Jean Ribault">Jean Ribault</a>'s column claiming Florida for <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a> in 1562.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>French <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huguenot" title="Huguenot">Huguenot</a> explorer <a href="/wiki/Jean_Ribault" title="Jean Ribault">Jean Ribault</a> charted the <a href="/wiki/St._Johns_River" title="St. Johns River">St. Johns River</a> in 1562, calling it the River of May because that was the month of his discovery. Ribault erected a stone column at the site, claiming the newly discovered land for <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">France</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> In 1564, <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Goulaine_de_Laudonni%C3%A8re" title="René Goulaine de Laudonnière">René Goulaine de Laudonnière</a> established the first European settlement, <b>Fort Caroline</b>, on the St. Johns near the main village of the Saturiwa. <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Spain" title="Philip II of Spain">Philip II of Spain</a> ordered <a href="/wiki/Pedro_Men%C3%A9ndez_de_Avil%C3%A9s" title="Pedro Menéndez de Avilés">Pedro Menéndez de Avilés</a> to protect the interest of <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> by attacking the French presence at Fort Caroline. On September 20, 1565, a Spanish force from the nearby Spanish settlement of <a href="/wiki/St._Augustine,_Florida" title="St. Augustine, Florida">St. Augustine</a> attacked <a href="/wiki/Fort_Caroline" title="Fort Caroline">Fort Caroline</a>, and killed nearly all the French soldiers defending it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spanish_people" title="Spanish people">Spanish</a> renamed the fort <i>San Mateo</i>, and following the ejection of the French, St. Augustine's position as the most important settlement in Florida was solidified. The location of Fort Caroline is subject to debate but a reconstruction of the fort was established on the St. Johns River in 1964.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Spain ceded Florida to the British in 1763 after the <a href="/wiki/French_and_Indian_War" title="French and Indian War">French and Indian War</a>, and the British soon constructed the King's Road connecting St. Augustine to <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Georgia" title="Province of Georgia">Georgia</a>. The road crossed the St. Johns River at a narrow point, which the <a href="/wiki/Seminole" title="Seminole">Seminole</a> called <i>Wacca Pilatka</i> and the British called the <b>Cow Ford</b>; these names ostensibly reflect the fact that <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a> were brought across the river there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The British introduced the cultivation of sugar cane, indigo and fruits, as well the export of lumber. As a result, the northeastern Florida area prospered economically more than it had under the Spanish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Britain ceded control of the territory to Spain in 1783, after being defeated in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>, and the settlement at the Cow Ford continued to grow. After Spain ceded the <a href="/wiki/Florida_Territory" title="Florida Territory">Florida Territory</a> to the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> in 1821, American settlers on the north side of the Cow Ford decided to plan a town, laying out the streets and plats. They soon named the town <b>Jacksonville</b>, after <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a>. Led by <a href="/wiki/Isaiah_Hart" title="Isaiah Hart">Isaiah D. Hart</a>, residents wrote a charter for a town government, which was approved by the Florida Legislative Council on February 9, 1832.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_War_and_the_Gilded_Age">Civil War and the Gilded Age</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacksonville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Civil War and the Gilded Age">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Guardhousejax1864.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="393" data-file-width="600" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Guardhousejax1864.jpg/220px-Guardhousejax1864.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Guardhousejax1864.jpg/330px-Guardhousejax1864.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Guardhousejax1864.jpg/440px-Guardhousejax1864.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Guardhousejax1864.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Union_Army" title="Union Army">Union Army</a> guard house on Bay Street in December 1864.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, Jacksonville was a key supply point for hogs and cattle being shipped from Florida to feed the <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate</a> forces. The city was blockaded by <a href="/wiki/Union_(American_Civil_War)" title="Union (American Civil War)">Union</a> forces, who gained control of nearby <a href="/wiki/Fort_Clinch" title="Fort Clinch">Fort Clinch</a>. Though no battles were fought in Jacksonville proper, the city changed hands several times between Union and Confederate forces. In the <a href="/wiki/Skirmish_of_the_Brick_Church" title="Skirmish of the Brick Church">Skirmish of the Brick Church</a> in 1862, Confederates won their first victory in the state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Metro_Jacksonville_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Metro_Jacksonville-28">[28]</a></sup> However, Union forces captured a Confederate position at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_St._Johns_Bluff" title="Battle of St. Johns Bluff">Battle of St. Johns Bluff</a>, and occupied Jacksonville in 1862. Slaves escaped to freedom in Union lines. In February 1864 Union forces left Jacksonville and confronted a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confederate_Army" title="Confederate Army">Confederate Army</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Olustee" title="Battle of Olustee">Battle of Olustee</a>, going down to defeat.</p>
<p>Union forces retreated to Jacksonville and held the city for the remainder of the war. In March 1864 a Confederate cavalry confronted a Union expedition in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cedar_Creek_(Jacksonville)" title="Battle of Cedar Creek (Jacksonville)">Battle of Cedar Creek</a>. Warfare and the long occupation left the city disrupted after the war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>During <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reconstruction_era_of_the_United_States" title="Reconstruction era of the United States">Reconstruction</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gilded_Age" title="Gilded Age">Gilded Age</a>, Jacksonville and nearby St. Augustine became popular winter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Resorts" title="Resorts">resorts</a> for the rich and famous. Visitors arrived by <a href="/wiki/Steamboat" title="Steamboat">steamboat</a> and later by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Railroad" title="Railroad">railroad</a>. President <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">Grover Cleveland</a> attended the Sub-Tropical Exposition in the city on February 22, 1888 during his trip to Florida.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> This highlighted the visibility of the state as a worthy place for tourism. The city's tourism, however, was dealt major blows in the late 19th century by <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">yellow fever</a> outbreaks. In addition, extension of the <a href="/wiki/Florida_East_Coast_Railway" title="Florida East Coast Railway">Florida East Coast Railway</a> further south drew visitors to other areas. From 1893 to 1938, Jacksonville was the site of the Florida Old Confederate <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_soldiers_home" title="Old soldiers home">Soldiers and Sailors Home</a>; it operated a nearby cemetery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Fire_of_1901">Great Fire of 1901</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacksonville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Great Fire of 1901">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Firejax1901.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="447" data-file-width="600" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Firejax1901.jpg/220px-Firejax1901.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Firejax1901.jpg/330px-Firejax1901.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Firejax1901.jpg/440px-Firejax1901.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Firejax1901.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ruins of the courthouse and armory from the <a href="/wiki/Great_Fire_of_1901" title="Great Fire of 1901">Great Fire of 1901</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On May 3, 1901, downtown Jacksonville was ravaged by a fire that started as a kitchen fire. Spanish moss at a nearby mattress factory was quickly engulfed in flames and enabled the fire to spread rapidly. In just eight hours, it swept through 146 city blocks, destroyed over 2,000 buildings, left about 10,000 homeless and killed seven residents. The Confederate Monument in <a href="/wiki/Hemming_Park" title="Hemming Park">Hemming Park</a> was one of the few landmarks to survive the fire. <a href="/wiki/William_Sherman_Jennings" title="William Sherman Jennings">Governor William Sherman Jennings</a> declared martial law and sent the state militia to maintain order; on May 17, municipal authority resumed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> It is said the glow from the flames could be seen in <a href="/wiki/Savannah,_Georgia" title="Savannah, Georgia">Savannah, Georgia</a>, and the smoke plumes seen in <a href="/wiki/Raleigh,_North_Carolina" title="Raleigh, North Carolina">Raleigh, North Carolina</a>. Known as the "<a href="/wiki/Great_Fire_of_1901" title="Great Fire of 1901">Great Fire of 1901</a>", it was one of the worst disasters in Florida history and the largest urban fire in the <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_United_States" title="Southeastern United States">southeastern United States</a>. Architect <a href="/wiki/Henry_John_Klutho" title="Henry John Klutho">Henry John Klutho</a> was a primary figure in the reconstruction of the city. The first multi-story structure built by Klutho was the <a href="/wiki/Dyal-Upchurch_Building" title="Dyal-Upchurch Building">Dyal-Upchurch Building</a> in 1902.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DYAL_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-DYAL-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JAH_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-JAH-34">[34]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/St._James_Building" title="St. James Building">St. James Building</a>, built on the previous site of the St. James Hotel that burned down, was built in 1912 as Klutho's crowning achievement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_Jacksonville">Modern Jacksonville</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacksonville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Modern Jacksonville">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JacksonvilleFL1914.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="484" data-file-width="600" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/JacksonvilleFL1914.jpg/220px-JacksonvilleFL1914.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/JacksonvilleFL1914.jpg/330px-JacksonvilleFL1914.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/JacksonvilleFL1914.jpg/440px-JacksonvilleFL1914.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:JacksonvilleFL1914.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Downtown Jacksonville in 1914</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the 1910s, New York–based filmmakers were attracted to Jacksonville's warm climate, exotic locations, excellent rail access, and cheap labor. Over the course of the decade, more than 30 <a href="/wiki/Silent_film" title="Silent film">silent film</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Movie_studio" title="Movie studio">studios</a> were established, earning Jacksonville the title of "Winter Film Capital of the World". However, the emergence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hollywood,_Los_Angeles,_California" title="Hollywood, Los Angeles, California">Hollywood</a> as a major film production center ended the city's film industry. One converted movie studio site, <a href="/wiki/Norman_Studios" title="Norman Studios">Norman Studios</a>, remains in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arlington,_Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Arlington, Jacksonville, Florida">Arlington</a>; it has been converted to the Jacksonville Silent Film Museum at Norman Studios.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-normanstudios_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-normanstudios-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>During this time, Jacksonville also became a banking and insurance center, with companies such as <a href="/wiki/Barnett_Bank" title="Barnett Bank">Barnett Bank</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_National_Bank" title="Atlantic National Bank">Atlantic National Bank</a>, <a href="/wiki/Florida_National_Bank" title="Florida National Bank">Florida National Bank</a>, <a href="/wiki/Prudential_Financial" title="Prudential Financial">Prudential</a>, Gulf Life, Afro-American Insurance, Independent Life and American Heritage Life thriving in the business district. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">U.S. Navy</a> became a major employer and economic force during the 1940s and the Second World War, constructing two Navy bases in the city and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps" title="United States Marine Corps">U.S. Marine Corps</a> establishing Blount Island Command.</p>
<p>Jacksonville, like most large cities in the United States, suffered from negative effects of rapid <a href="/wiki/Urban_sprawl" title="Urban sprawl">urban sprawl</a> after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. The construction of federal highways was a kind of subsidy that enabled development of suburban housing, and wealthier, better established residents moved to newer housing in the suburbs. After World War II, the government of the city of Jacksonville began to increase spending to fund new public building projects in the postwar economic boom. Mayor <a href="/wiki/W._Haydon_Burns" title="W. Haydon Burns">W. Haydon Burns</a>' <i>Jacksonville Story</i> resulted in the construction of a new city hall, civic auditorium, public library and other projects that created a dynamic sense of civic pride. However, the development of suburbs and a subsequent wave of middle class "<a href="/wiki/White_flight" title="White flight">white flight</a>" resulted in a remaining population with a higher level of poverty overall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-census_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-census-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>Given the postwar migration of residents, businesses, and jobs, the city's tax base declined. It had difficulty funding education, sanitation, and traffic control within the city limits. In addition, residents in unincorporated suburbs had difficulty obtaining municipal services, such as sewage and building code enforcement. In 1958, a study recommended that the city of Jacksonville begin annexing outlying communities in order to create the needed larger geographic tax base to improve services throughout the county. Voters outside the city limits rejected annexation plans in six referendums between 1960 and 1965. The city's largest ethnic group, non-Hispanic white,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-census_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-census-37">[37]</a></sup> declined from 75.8% of the population in 1970 to 55.1% by 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-census1_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-census1-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jax_Consolidation_Headline_1967.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="259" data-file-width="384" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Jax_Consolidation_Headline_1967.jpg/220px-Jax_Consolidation_Headline_1967.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Jax_Consolidation_Headline_1967.jpg/330px-Jax_Consolidation_Headline_1967.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/9/96/Jax_Consolidation_Headline_1967.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jax_Consolidation_Headline_1967.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
News of Jacksonville's consolidation from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Florida_Times-Union" title="The Florida Times-Union">The Florida Times-Union</a></i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On December 29, 1963 the <a href="/wiki/Hotel_Roosevelt_fire" title="Hotel Roosevelt fire">Hotel Roosevelt fire</a> killed 22 people, the highest one-day death toll in Jacksonville.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> On September 10, 1964, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Dora" title="Hurricane Dora">Hurricane Dora</a> made landfall near <a href="/wiki/St._Augustine,_Florida" title="St. Augustine, Florida">St. Augustine</a>, causing major damage to buildings in North Florida. Hurricane Dora was the first hurricane to make a direct hit to North Florida.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the mid 1960s, corruption scandals arose among city officials, who were mainly part of a traditional conservative Democratic, white old boy network that had dominated politics for decades. After a <a href="/wiki/Grand_jury" title="Grand jury">grand jury</a> was convened to investigate, 11 officials were indicted and more were forced to resign.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Jacksonville_Consolidation" title="Jacksonville Consolidation">Jacksonville Consolidation</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/J._J._Daniel" title="J. J. Daniel">J. J. Daniel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Claude_Yates" title="Claude Yates">Claude Yates</a>, began to win more support during this period, from both inner-city blacks, who wanted more involvement in government after passage of civil rights legislation restored their ability to vote, and whites in the suburbs, who wanted more services and more control over the central city. In 1964 all 15 of Duval County's public high schools lost their accreditation. This added momentum to proposals for government reform. Lower taxes, increased economic development, unification of the community, better public spending, and effective administration by a more central authority were all cited as reasons for a new consolidated government.</p>
<p>When a <a href="/wiki/Consolidated_city-county" title="Consolidated city-county">consolidation</a> referendum was held in 1967, voters approved the plan. On October 1, 1968, the city and county governments merged to create the Consolidated City of Jacksonville. Fire, police, health &amp; welfare, recreation, public works, and housing &amp; urban development were all combined under the new government. In honor of the occasion, then-Mayor Hans Tanzler posed with actress <a href="/wiki/Lee_Meredith" title="Lee Meredith">Lee Meredith</a> behind a sign marking the new border of the "Bold New City of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_South" title="American South">South</a>" at Florida 13 and Julington Creek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> The consolidation created a 900 square mile entity.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Better_Jacksonville_Plan" title="Better Jacksonville Plan">Better Jacksonville Plan</a>, promoted as a <i>blueprint for Jacksonville's future</i> and approved by Jacksonville voters in 2000, authorized a half-penny sales tax. This would generate most of the revenue required for the $2.25 billion package of major projects that included road &amp; infrastructure improvements, environmental preservation, targeted economic development and new or improved public facilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NFL" title="NFL">NFL</a> awarded Jacksonville an NFL franchise called the <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville_Jaguars" title="Jacksonville Jaguars">Jacksonville Jaguars</a> on November 30, 1993.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> In 2005, Jacksonville hosted <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl_XXXIX" title="Super Bowl XXXIX">Super Bowl XXXIX</a> that was seen by an estimated 86 million viewers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ratings20090118_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-ratings20090118-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>In October 2016, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Matthew" title="Hurricane Matthew">Hurricane Matthew</a> caused major flooding and damage to Jacksonville, <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville_Beach,_Florida" title="Jacksonville Beach, Florida">Jacksonville Beach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Beach,_Florida" title="Atlantic Beach, Florida">Atlantic Beach</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neptune_Beach,_Florida" title="Neptune Beach, Florida">Neptune Beach</a>, the first such damage in the area since 2004.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> In September 2017, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Irma" title="Hurricane Irma">Hurricane Irma</a> caused record breaking floods in Jacksonville not seen since 1846.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacksonville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census_Bureau" title="United States Census Bureau">United States Census Bureau</a>, the city has a total area of 874.3 square miles (2,264 km<sup>2</sup>), making Jacksonville the largest city in land area in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continental_United_States" title="Continental United States">contiguous United States</a>; of this, 86.66% (757.7 sq mi or 1,962 km<sup>2</sup>) is land and 13.34% (116.7 sq mi or 302 km<sup>2</sup>) is water. Jacksonville surrounds the town of <a href="/wiki/Baldwin,_Florida" title="Baldwin, Florida">Baldwin</a>. <a href="/wiki/Nassau_County,_Florida" title="Nassau County, Florida">Nassau County</a> lies to the north, <a href="/wiki/Baker_County,_Florida" title="Baker County, Florida">Baker County</a> lies to the west, and <a href="/wiki/Clay_County,_Florida" title="Clay County, Florida">Clay</a> and <a href="/wiki/St._Johns_County,_Florida" title="St. Johns County, Florida">St. Johns County</a> lie to the south; the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> lies to the east, along with the <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville_Beach,_Florida" title="Jacksonville Beach, Florida">Jacksonville Beaches</a>. The <a href="/wiki/St._Johns_River" title="St. Johns River">St. Johns River</a> divides the city. The <a href="/wiki/Trout_River_(Florida)" title="Trout River (Florida)">Trout River</a>, a major tributary of the St. Johns River, is located entirely within Jacksonville.</p>
<p>The state of Florida, including Jacksonville, is a huge flat plateau with a high water table, and surface lakes are very shallow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">United States Geological Survey</a> states that the highest point in Jacksonville is only 40 feet (12.2 meters) above sea level, making the area susceptible to flooding and <a href="/wiki/Storm_surge" title="Storm surge">storm surge</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Soil composition is primarily sand and clay rather than limestone, so very few <a href="/wiki/Sinkhole" title="Sinkhole">sinkholes</a> develop; however deep, large diameter sinkholes do occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cityscape">Cityscape</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacksonville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Cityscape">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:1608px;">
<div class="thumbinner" style="">
<div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:JaxpanoAcosta.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="1132" data-file-width="6096" height="297" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/JaxpanoAcosta.png/1600px-JaxpanoAcosta.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/JaxpanoAcosta.png/2400px-JaxpanoAcosta.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/JaxpanoAcosta.png/3200px-JaxpanoAcosta.png 2x" width="1600"/></a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:1608px;">
<div class="thumbinner" style="">
<div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jacksonville_Acosta_Bridge_Panorama.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="16309" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Jacksonville_Acosta_Bridge_Panorama.jpg/1600px-Jacksonville_Acosta_Bridge_Panorama.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Jacksonville_Acosta_Bridge_Panorama.jpg/2400px-Jacksonville_Acosta_Bridge_Panorama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Jacksonville_Acosta_Bridge_Panorama.jpg/3200px-Jacksonville_Acosta_Bridge_Panorama.jpg 2x" width="1600"/></a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacksonville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Jacksonville" title="Architecture of Jacksonville">Architecture of Jacksonville</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_in_Jacksonville" title="List of tallest buildings in Jacksonville">List of tallest buildings in Jacksonville</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Jacksonville" title="Architecture of Jacksonville">architecture of Jacksonville</a> varies in style. Few structures in the city center predate the <a href="/wiki/Great_Fire_of_1901" title="Great Fire of 1901">Great Fire of 1901</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Metrojacksonville2_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Metrojacksonville2-51">[51]</a></sup> The city is home to one of the largest collections of <a href="/wiki/Prairie_School" title="Prairie School">Prairie School</a> style buildings outside the Midwest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pst_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-pst-52">[52]</a></sup> following the Great Fire of 1901, <a href="/wiki/Henry_John_Klutho" title="Henry John Klutho">Henry John Klutho</a> would come to influence generations of local designers with his works by both the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_school_(architecture)" title="Chicago school (architecture)">Chicago School</a>, championed by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Sullivan" title="Louis Sullivan">Louis Sullivan</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Prairie_School" title="Prairie School">Prairie School</a> of architecture, popularized by <a href="/wiki/Frank_Lloyd_Wright" title="Frank Lloyd Wright">Frank Lloyd Wright</a>. Jacksonville is also home to a notable collection of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mid-Century_modern" title="Mid-Century modern">Mid-Century modern</a> architecture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jhs_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jhs-53">[53]</a></sup> Local architects <a href="/wiki/Robert_C._Broward" title="Robert C. Broward">Robert C. Broward</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taylor_Hardwick" title="Taylor Hardwick">Taylor Hardwick</a>, and <a href="/wiki/William_Morgan_(architect)" title="William Morgan (architect)">William Morgan</a> adapted a range design principles, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_style_(architecture)" title="International style (architecture)">International style</a>, Brutalism, Futurism and Organicism, all applied with an American interpretation generally referred to today as Mid-century modern design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jhs_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jhs-53">[53]</a></sup> The architecture firms of <a href="/wiki/Reynolds,_Smith_%26_Hills" title="Reynolds, Smith &amp; Hills">Reynolds, Smith &amp; Hills</a> (RS&amp;H)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mj3_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-mj3-54">[54]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/KBJ_Architects" title="KBJ Architects">Kemp, Bunch &amp; Jackson</a> (KBJ) have also contributed a number of important works to the city's modern architectural movement.</p>
<p>Jacksonville's early predominant position as a regional center of business left an indelible mark on the city's skyline. Many of the earliest skyscrapers in the state were constructed in Jacksonville, dating to 1902.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Metrojacksonville_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Metrojacksonville-55">[55]</a></sup> The city last held the state height record from 1974 to 1981.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emp_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-emp-56">[56]</a></sup> The tallest building in Downtown Jacksonville's <a href="/wiki/Skyline" title="Skyline">skyline</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_America_Tower_(Jacksonville)" title="Bank of America Tower (Jacksonville)">Bank of America Tower</a>, constructed in 1990 as the Barnett Center. It has a height of 617 ft (188 m) and includes 42 floors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SKY_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-SKY-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PRR_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-PRR-58">[58]</a></sup> Other notable structures include the 37-story <a href="/wiki/Wells_Fargo_Center_(Jacksonville)" title="Wells Fargo Center (Jacksonville)">Wells Fargo Center</a> (with its distinctive flared base making it the defining building in the Jacksonville skyline),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> originally built in 1972-74 by the Independent Life and Accident Insurance Company, and the 28-floor <a href="/wiki/Riverplace_Tower" title="Riverplace Tower">Riverplace Tower</a> . When completed in 1967, it was the tallest precast, <a href="/wiki/Prestressed_concrete" title="Prestressed concrete">post-tensioned concrete</a> structure in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>

<p>Like much of the south Atlantic region of the United States, Jacksonville has a <a href="/wiki/Humid_subtropical_climate" title="Humid subtropical climate">humid subtropical climate</a> (Köppen <i>Cfa</i>), with mild weather during winters and hot and humid weather during summers. Seasonal rainfall is concentrated in the warmest months from May through September, while the driest months are from November through April. Rainfall averages around 52 inches (1,300 mm) a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Due to Jacksonville's low latitude and coastal location, the city has infrequent cold weather, and winters are typically mild and sunny. Summer thunderstorms with torrential but brief downpours are common.</p>
<p>Mean monthly temperatures range from around 53 °F (12 °C) in January to 82 °F (28 °C) in July. High temperatures average 64 to 92 °F (18 to 33 °C) throughout the year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> High <a href="/wiki/Heat_index" title="Heat index">heat indices</a> are common for the summer months in the area, with indices above 110 °F (43.3 °C) possible. The highest temperature recorded was 104 °F (40 °C) on July 11, 1879 and July 28, 1872.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Extremes_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Extremes-65">[65]</a></sup> It is common for thunderstorms to erupt during a typical summer afternoon. These are caused by the rapid heating of the land relative to the water, combined with extremely high humidity.</p>
<p>During winter, there can be hard freezes during the night. Such cold weather is usually short lived, as the city averages only 10 to 15 nights at or below freezing and around 5 days where the high does not rise above 50 °F (10 °C).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> The coldest temperature recorded at <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville_International_Airport" title="Jacksonville International Airport">Jacksonville International Airport</a> was 7 °F (−14 °C) on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/January_1985_Arctic_outbreak" title="January 1985 Arctic outbreak">January 21, 1985</a>. Jacksonville has recorded three days with measurable snow since 1911, most recently a one-inch (2.5 cm) snowfall in December 1989<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> and flurries in December 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>Jacksonville has suffered less damage from <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">hurricanes</a> than most other east coast cities, although the threat does exist for a direct hit by a major hurricane. The city has only received one direct hit from a hurricane since 1871; however, Jacksonville has experienced hurricane or near-hurricane conditions more than a dozen times due to storms crossing the state from the Gulf of Mexico to the Atlantic Ocean, or passing to the north or south in the Atlantic and brushing past the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The strongest effect on Jacksonville was from <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Dora" title="Hurricane Dora">Hurricane Dora</a> in 1964, the only recorded storm to hit the First Coast with sustained hurricane-force winds. The eye crossed St. Augustine with winds that had just barely diminished to 110 mph (180 km/h), making it a strong Category 2 on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saffir%E2%80%93Simpson_Hurricane_Scale" title="Saffir–Simpson Hurricane Scale">Saffir-Simpson Scale</a>. Jacksonville also suffered damage from 2008's <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Storm_Fay_(2008)" title="Tropical Storm Fay (2008)">Tropical Storm Fay</a> which crisscrossed the state, bringing parts of Jacksonville under darkness for four days. Similarly, four years prior to this, Jacksonville was inundated by <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Frances" title="Hurricane Frances">Hurricane Frances</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Jeanne" title="Hurricane Jeanne">Hurricane Jeanne</a>, which made landfall south of the area. These tropical cyclones were the costliest indirect hits to Jacksonville. <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Floyd" title="Hurricane Floyd">Hurricane Floyd</a> in 1999 caused damage mainly to Jacksonville Beach. During Floyd, the Jacksonville Beach pier was severely damaged, and later demolished. The rebuilt pier was later damaged by Fay, but not destroyed. <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Storm_Bonnie_(2004)" title="Tropical Storm Bonnie (2004)">Tropical Storm Bonnie</a> would cause minor damage in 2004, spawning a minor tornado in the process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> On May 28, 2012, Jacksonville was hit by <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Storm_Beryl_(2012)" title="Tropical Storm Beryl (2012)">Tropical Storm Beryl</a>, packing winds up to 70 miles per hour (113 km/h) which made landfall near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jacksonville_Beach" title="Jacksonville Beach">Jacksonville Beach</a>.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parks">Parks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacksonville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Parks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The City of Jacksonville has a unique park system, with various lands operated by the <a href="/wiki/National_Park_Service" title="National Park Service">National Park Service</a>, <a href="/wiki/Florida_State_Parks" title="Florida State Parks">Florida State Parks</a> and the City of Jacksonville Department of Parks and Recreation. Jacksonville operates the largest urban park system in the United States, providing facilities and services at more than 337 locations on more than 80,000 acres (320 km<sup>2</sup>) located throughout the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[74]</a></sup> Jacksonville enjoys natural beauty from the St. Johns River and Atlantic Ocean. Many parks provide access for people to boat, swim, fish, sail, jetski, surf and waterski. Several parks around the city have received international recognition.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="National_parks">National parks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacksonville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: National parks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/National_Park_Service" title="National Park Service">National Park Service</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KingsleyPlantationSunset2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="315" data-file-width="720" height="96" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/KingsleyPlantationSunset2.jpg/220px-KingsleyPlantationSunset2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/KingsleyPlantationSunset2.jpg/330px-KingsleyPlantationSunset2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/KingsleyPlantationSunset2.jpg/440px-KingsleyPlantationSunset2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KingsleyPlantationSunset2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Kingsley_Plantation" title="Kingsley Plantation">Kingsley Plantation</a>, located within the <a href="/wiki/Timucuan_Preserve" title="Timucuan Preserve">Timucuan Preserve</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Timucuan_Preserve" title="Timucuan Preserve">Timucuan Preserve</a> is a U.S. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Preserve" title="National Preserve">National Preserve</a> comprising over 46,000 acres (19,000 ha) of <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetlands</a> and waterways. It includes natural and historic areas such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fort_Caroline_National_Memorial" title="Fort Caroline National Memorial">Fort Caroline National Memorial</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kingsley_Plantation" title="Kingsley Plantation">Kingsley Plantation</a>, the oldest standing plantation in the state.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="State_parks">State parks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacksonville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: State parks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Florida_State_Parks" title="Florida State Parks">Florida State Parks</a></div>
<p>There are several state parks within the city limits of Jacksonville, these include <a href="/wiki/Amelia_Island_State_Park" title="Amelia Island State Park">Amelia Island State Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Big_Talbot_Island_State_Park" title="Big Talbot Island State Park">Big Talbot Island State Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fort_George_Island_Cultural_State_Park" title="Fort George Island Cultural State Park">Fort George Island Cultural State Park</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Crady_Bridge_Fishing_Pier_State_Park" title="George Crady Bridge Fishing Pier State Park">George Crady Bridge Fishing Pier State Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Little_Talbot_Island_State_Park" title="Little Talbot Island State Park">Little Talbot Island State Park</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pumpkin_Hill_Creek_Preserve_State_Park" title="Pumpkin Hill Creek Preserve State Park">Pumpkin Hill Creek Preserve State Park</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Bluff_Fort_Historic_State_Park" title="Yellow Bluff Fort Historic State Park">Yellow Bluff Fort Historic State Park</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="City_parks">City parks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacksonville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: City parks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_parks_in_Jacksonville" title="List of parks in Jacksonville">List of parks in Jacksonville</a></div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Confederate_Park_(Jacksonville)" title="Confederate Park (Jacksonville)">Confederate Park</a> is a public park on the southern bounds of the historic neighborhood of <a href="/wiki/Springfield_(Jacksonville)" title="Springfield (Jacksonville)">Springfield</a>, and is part of a network of parks that parallel Hogans Creek. The park opened in 1907 as Dignan Park, named for a former chairman of the city's Board of Public Works. In 1914, the park hosted the annual reunion of the <a href="/wiki/United_Confederate_Veterans" title="United Confederate Veterans">United Confederate Veterans</a>, a gathering of former <a href="/wiki/Confederate_States_of_America" title="Confederate States of America">Confederate</a> soldiers. Five months after the reunion the city renamed the park "Confederate Park." A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confederate_monument" title="Confederate monument">Confederate monument</a> was erected in 1915 honoring the Women of the Southland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[75]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:1108px;">
<div class="thumbinner" style="">
<div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Confederatepano.jpg" title="View of downtown, as seen from Confederate Park"><img alt="" data-file-height="1366" data-file-width="9568" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Confederatepano.jpg/1100px-Confederatepano.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Confederatepano.jpg/1650px-Confederatepano.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Confederatepano.jpg/2200px-Confederatepano.jpg 2x" width="1100"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Confederatepano.jpg" title="File:Confederatepano.jpg"></a></div>
View of <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Jacksonville" title="Downtown Jacksonville">downtown</a>, as seen from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Confederate_Park_(Jacksonville,_Florida)" title="Confederate Park (Jacksonville, Florida)">Confederate Park</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Friendship_Fountain" title="Friendship Fountain">Friendship Fountain</a> is a large <a href="/wiki/Fountain" title="Fountain">fountain</a> in St. Johns River Park at the west end of <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Jacksonville" title="Downtown Jacksonville">Downtown Jacksonville</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jacksonville_Riverwalk" title="Jacksonville Riverwalk">Southbank Riverwalk</a>. It opened in 1965 as the world's largest and tallest fountain, and has been one of Jacksonville's most recognizable and popular attractions. The fountain's three pumps could push 17,000 US gallons (64,000 L) of water per minute up to 100 feet (30 m) in height. Designed by Jacksonville architect <a href="/wiki/Taylor_Hardwick" title="Taylor Hardwick">Taylor Hardwick</a> in 1963 and, in 2011 the city completed a $3.2 million renovation to the fountain and the surrounding park. It features a light show and music each evening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hanna_Park" title="Hanna Park">Hanna Park</a> is a 1.5-mile (2.4 km) public <a href="/wiki/Beach" title="Beach">beach</a> and city park near Mayport in the <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville_Beaches" title="Jacksonville Beaches">Jacksonville Beaches</a> area. It consists of 447 acres (1.81 km<sup>2</sup>) of mature <a href="/wiki/Coast" title="Coast">coastal</a> <a href="/wiki/Hammock_(ecology)" title="Hammock (ecology)">hammock</a>, and was known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manhattan_Beach_(Florida)" title="Manhattan Beach (Florida)">Manhattan Beach</a>, Florida's first beach community for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African Americans</a> during the period of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Segregation_in_the_United_States" title="Segregation in the United States">segregation in the United States</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[77]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HannaParkBoardwalk.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="529" data-file-width="800" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/HannaParkBoardwalk.JPG/220px-HannaParkBoardwalk.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/HannaParkBoardwalk.JPG/330px-HannaParkBoardwalk.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/HannaParkBoardwalk.JPG/440px-HannaParkBoardwalk.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HannaParkBoardwalk.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Hanna_Park" title="Hanna Park">Hanna Park</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hemming_Park" title="Hemming Park">Hemming Park</a> is a 1.54-acre (6,200 m<sup>2</sup>) public park in the heart of the government center in downtown. Originally a <a href="/wiki/Village_green" title="Village green">village green</a>, it was the first and is the oldest park in the city. The area was established as a public square in 1857 by <a href="/wiki/Isaiah_Hart" title="Isaiah Hart">Isaiah Hart</a>, founder of Jacksonville. The first Wednesday of every month, Hemming Park is converted into the centerpiece of Jacksonville's Downtown Art Walk. The third Thursday of every month Hemming Park hosts a night market called Jaxsons Night Market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[78]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_J._Klutho_Park" title="Henry J. Klutho Park">Klutho Park</a> is an 18.34-acre (74,200 m<sup>2</sup>) public park, between downtownand the historic neighborhood of <a href="/wiki/Springfield_(Jacksonville)" title="Springfield (Jacksonville)">Springfield</a>. It is part of a network of parks that parallel Hogans Creek, Klutho Park being the largest. Created between 1899 and 1901 on land donated by the Springfield Company. The park also housed the City’s first zoo, opening at the park in 1914. The Hogans Creek Improvement Project of 1929–30, designed by architect <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henry_J._Klutho" title="Henry J. Klutho">Henry J. Klutho</a>, turned much of the park grounds into a Venetian-style promenade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[79]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jacksonville-Baldwin_Rail_Trail" title="Jacksonville-Baldwin Rail Trail">Jacksonville-Baldwin Rail Trail</a> is a 14.5-mile (23.3 km) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rail_Trail" title="Rail Trail">Rail Trail</a> that extends northwest to <a href="/wiki/Baldwin,_Florida" title="Baldwin, Florida">Baldwin, Florida</a>. It includes three separate paths; a multi-use asphalt trail for hiking, jogging, in-line skating or cycling; an off-road bike trail; and a horseback riding trail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[80]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jessie_Ball_DuPont_Park" title="Jessie Ball DuPont Park">Jessie Ball DuPont Park</a> is a 7-acre (2.8 ha) park, home to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_Oak_(Jacksonville,_Florida)" title="Treaty Oak (Jacksonville, Florida)">Treaty Oak</a>, a massive 250-year-old tree in the Southbank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[81]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Park" title="Metropolitan Park">Metropolitan Park</a> is a 32-acre (130,000 m<sup>2</sup>) waterfront park on the <a href="/wiki/St._Johns_River" title="St. Johns River">St. Johns River</a>, in the Sports Complex area of downtown. The multi-purpose facility contains an exhibition area, picnic and playground area, and a performance pavilion which has a capacity of 10,000 persons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[82]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Memorial_Park_(Jacksonville)" title="Memorial Park (Jacksonville)">Memorial Park</a> is a 5.85-acre (23,700 m<sup>2</sup>) public park, on the <a href="/wiki/St._Johns_River" title="St. Johns River">St. Johns River</a> in the historic neighborhoods <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riverside,_Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Riverside, Jacksonville, Florida">Riverside</a>. Completed in 1924, it is the third oldest park in the city. Built to honor of the 1,200 Floridians who died serving during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, the notable <a href="/wiki/Olmsted_Brothers" title="Olmsted Brothers">Olmsted Brothers</a> were commissioned to design the park, along with local architect <a href="/wiki/Roy_A._Benjamin" title="Roy A. Benjamin">Roy A. Benjamin</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_Adrian_Pillars" title="Charles Adrian Pillars">Charles Adrian Pillars</a> designed the bronze sculpture, 'Life', prominently showcased in the park.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[83]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Riverside_Park_(Jacksonville)" title="Riverside Park (Jacksonville)">Riverside Park</a> is an 11.4-acre (46,000 m<sup>2</sup>) public park, in the historic neighborhood of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riverside,_Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Riverside, Jacksonville, Florida">Riverside</a>. It is the second oldest park in the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[84]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jacksonville_Riverwalk" title="Jacksonville Riverwalk">Riverwalk</a> 2.0 miles (3.2 km) along the St. Johns from Berkman Plaza to I-95 at the Fuller Warren Bridge while the Southbank Riverwalk stretches 1.2 miles (1.9 km) from the Radisson Hotel to Museum Circle. The <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville_Landing" title="Jacksonville Landing">Jacksonville Landing</a> is a popular riverfront dining and shopping venue, accessible by River Taxi from the Southbank Riverwalk. Adjacent to Museum Circle is St. Johns River Park, also known as Friendship Park. It is the location of <a href="/wiki/Friendship_Fountain" title="Friendship Fountain">Friendship Fountain</a>, one of the most recognizable and popular attractions in Jacksonville. This landmark was built in 1965 and promoted as the "World's Tallest and Largest" fountain at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[85]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Veterans_Memorial_Wall" title="Veterans Memorial Wall">Veterans Memorial Wall</a> is a tribute to local servicemen and women killed while serving in US armed forces. A ceremony is held each Memorial Day recognizing any service woman or man from Jacksonville who died in the previous year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[86]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacksonville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Evergreen_Cemetery_(Jacksonville,_Florida)" title="Evergreen Cemetery (Jacksonville, Florida)">Evergreen Cemetery</a> is a large historic cemetery added to the <a href="/wiki/National_Register_of_Historic_Places" title="National Register of Historic Places">National Register of Historic Places</a> on April 8, 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-evergreenjax_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-evergreenjax-89">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-grave_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-grave-90">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-metro_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-metro-91">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jacksonville_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-jacksonville-92">[90]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jacksonville_Arboretum_%26_Gardens" title="Jacksonville Arboretum &amp; Gardens">Jacksonville Arboretum &amp; Gardens</a> broke ground on a new center in April 2007 and held their grand opening on November 15, 2008.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jacksonville_Zoo_and_Gardens" title="Jacksonville Zoo and Gardens">Jacksonville Zoo and Gardens</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jacksonville_National_Cemetery" title="Jacksonville National Cemetery">Jacksonville National Cemetery</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Old_City_Cemetery_(Jacksonville,_Florida)" title="Old City Cemetery (Jacksonville, Florida)">Old City Cemetery</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tree_Hill_Nature_Center" title="Tree Hill Nature Center">Tree Hill Nature Center</a> is a nature preserve and environmental education center located five minutes from Downtown Jacksonville.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Neighborhoods">Neighborhoods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacksonville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Neighborhoods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neighborhoods_of_Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Neighborhoods of Jacksonville, Florida">Neighborhoods of Jacksonville, Florida</a></div>
<p>There are more than 500 neighborhoods within Jacksonville's vast area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CPACS_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-CPACS-93">[91]</a></sup> These include <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Jacksonville" title="Downtown Jacksonville">Downtown Jacksonville</a> and its surrounding neighborhoods, including <a href="/wiki/LaVilla" title="LaVilla">LaVilla</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brooklyn,_Jacksonville" title="Brooklyn, Jacksonville">Brooklyn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Riverside_and_Avondale" title="Riverside and Avondale">Riverside and Avondale</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Springfield,_Jacksonville" title="Springfield, Jacksonville">Springfield</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastside_(Jacksonville)" title="Eastside (Jacksonville)">Eastside</a>, and <a href="/wiki/San_Marco_(Jacksonville)" title="San Marco (Jacksonville)">San Marco</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[92]</a></sup> Additionally, greater Jacksonville is traditionally divided into several amorphous areas, comprising large parts of Duval County. These are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northside,_Jacksonville" title="Northside, Jacksonville">Northside</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Westside,_Jacksonville," title="Westside, Jacksonville,">Westside</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southside,_Jacksonville" title="Southside, Jacksonville">Southside</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arlington,_Jacksonville" title="Arlington, Jacksonville">Arlington</a>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville_Beaches" title="Jacksonville Beaches">Jacksonville Beaches</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McEwen_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-McEwen-95">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are four municipalities that have retained their own governments since consolidation; these are <a href="/wiki/Baldwin,_Florida" title="Baldwin, Florida">Baldwin</a> and the three Jacksonville Beaches towns of <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Beach,_Florida" title="Atlantic Beach, Florida">Atlantic Beach</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neptune_Beach,_Florida" title="Neptune Beach, Florida">Neptune Beach</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jacksonville_Beach,_Florida" title="Jacksonville Beach, Florida">Jacksonville Beach</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[94]</a></sup> Four of Jacksonville's neighborhoods, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avondale,_Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Avondale, Jacksonville, Florida">Avondale</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ortega,_Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Ortega, Jacksonville, Florida">Ortega</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Springfield,_Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Springfield, Jacksonville, Florida">Springfield</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riverside,_Jacksonville,_Florida" title="Riverside, Jacksonville, Florida">Riverside</a>, have been identified as U.S. <a href="/wiki/Historic_district" title="Historic district">historic districts</a> and are in the <a href="/wiki/National_Register_of_Historic_Places" title="National Register of Historic Places">National Register of Historic Places</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nrhp_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-nrhp-97">[95]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Jacksonville,_Florida&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<p>Jacksonville is the most populous city in Florida, and the twelfth most populous city in the United States. As of 2010<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Jacksonville,_Florida&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, there were 821,784 people and 366,273 households in the city. Jacksonville has the country's tenth-largest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">Arab</a> population, with a total population of 5,751 according to the 2000 United States Census.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[101]</a></sup> Jacksonville has Florida's largest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Filipino_American" title="Filipino American">Filipino American</a> community, with 25,033 in the metropolitan area as of the 2010 Census. Much of Jacksonville's Filipino community served in or has ties to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Navy" title="United States Navy">United States Navy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JAX2009_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-JAX2009-104">[102]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Race_and_ethnicity_2010-_Jacksonville_(5559898547).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3009" data-file-width="3009" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Race_and_ethnicity_2010-_Jacksonville_%285559898547%29.png/220px-Race_and_ethnicity_2010-_Jacksonville_%285559898547%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Race_and_ethnicity_2010-_Jacksonville_%285559898547%29.png/330px-Race_and_ethnicity_2010-_Jacksonville_%285559898547%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Race_and_ethnicity_2010-_Jacksonville_%285559898547%29.png/440px-Race_and_ethnicity_2010-_Jacksonville_%285559898547%29.png 2x" width="220"/></a>

Map of racial distribution in Jacksonville, 2010 U.S. Census. Each dot is 25 people: <span style="color:#ff0000"><b>White</b></span>, <span style="color:#0000ff"><b>Black</b></span>, <span style="color:#00ff80"><b>Asian</b></span> <span style="color:#ff8000"><b>Hispanic</b></span>, or <b>Other</b> (yellow)</div>
